POINTE-A-PITRE, GuadeloupeÂ»Auff.
The San Francisco Call.
13. â The hurricane spread ruin and
death through these islands. In Orand
Terre fifty deaths and 280 injured have
been reported thus far. ThÂ» towns of
Le Monle, Port Louis and St. Francois
are devastated.
Pointe-a-Pitre is greatly damaged,
the estimated loss being $3,000,000. Six
steamers and twenty-two sehcroners
were sunk or damaged beyond repair.
A great number of fishing craft were
lost.
Communcation with the interor is
impossible, but courier reports reveal
frightful damage. The coffee and cocoa
crops .'ire ruined and sugar cane is
practically destroyed.
A daring spectator f>f the storm was
Frances Drake, an American news
paper woman, who was seen for hours
standing on a pier 300 feet out in the
ocean.
The whole of Desirade Island was
devastated. People are starving and
without shelter.

PEOPLE OF PONCE
BURYING THEIR DEAD
PONCE, Porto Rico. Aug. 13.âAl
though the disasters which followed the
hurricane have not been overesti
mated, the people are hopeful and en
ring to make the best of the situ
ation. Dead bodies are buried where
they are found.
Food suppli-s are b r -ing distributed
and repairs to bridges and roads are
being pushed forward under military
supervision, with payments to work
men daily.
It is gathered from interviews with
merchants and planters, some of whom
owe European houses, that there will
be numerous failures.
The steamer Australia, with cargo.
v is wrecked during the hurricane off
Arroyo on the southeast coast, and tho
Vasco off Arecibo, on the north coast.
HURRICANE SEEMS
TO BE DECREASING
WASHINGTON, Aug. 13.â The West
Tndian hurricane appears to be gradu
ally decreasing in strength, and the
chances are that it will spend itself be
fore making much further progress.
This is the welcome news given out by
the ofiieials of the weather bureau to
night. It is based on the fact that there
has not been any decided fall in the
barometer In the localities that should
feel its effect, and by the presence of
an area of high pressure in the north
ern part of the United States which
tends to keep the storm from continu
ing on its onward march. The move
ment of the hurricane is apparently
very slow, and even at Jacksonville the
velocity of the wind this morning was
but twenty-one miles an hour. The
opinion of the officials is that by the
time Charleston is reached there will be
no more than an ordinary blow. Wil
mington. N. C, and Norfolk have taken
down their hurricane signals. The
storm now appears to he on the east
coast of Florida, and the latest reports
from Jupiter, dated at 4 o'clock this
morning, indicate a pretty severe blow
at that place. It is now south of Jack
sonville, indicating' a slow northward
movement since last night. The bureau
has had no wire reports south of Titus
ville, Fla.. some distance below Jack
sonville, sine.- last night, and nothing
from Nassau, Bahama Islands, since
Friday afternoon.

RENXES, Aug. 13. â To-morrow's ses
sion of the Dreyfus court-martial is
awaited with the greatest anxiety and
I expectation, in view of the confronta
i tion of Mercier by Casimir-Perier, when
the former will virtually be in the pris
oner's dock. A dramatic scene is inev
itable.
It is understood that an even more
thrilling incident than that which
ended yesterday's session was expected
by the counsel of Dreyfus and would
have occurred but for General Mer
cier's prudence. Dreyfus intended and
had actually declared his determination
to slap Mercier's face before the whole
court if he brought up the story of th
relations of Dreyfus with Mme. 8.,
whom some newspapers have asserted
acted as a go-between for Dreyfus and
the attache to whom he is alleged to
have made treasonable revelations.
General -Mercier abstained from ref
erence to the story, but despite this the
people in the court thought for a mo
ment that Dreyfus was about to strike
Mercier, until a captain of gendarmes
took him gently by the arm and pushed
him hack into his seat.
The Petit Journal and some other
papers, including: those published here,
accused the foreign press men present
in the court of being the authors of the
demonstration against Mercier when he
left the court. The accusation is ridic
ulous and was prompted by the obvious
desire of the anti-revisionists to ex
clude foreign newspaper men from
what they maintain is purely a French
matter. It was even stated that meas
ures would be taken against the for
eign correspondents. About sixty of the
latter met this evening to protest
against the charge. During the course
.>f the proceedings, Mme. Severine, the
i famous woman journalist, came on be
half of the committee of the French ju
diciary press, who had just held a
meeting on the subject and communi
cated the fact that no measures against
the foreign press would be taken or
were contemplated and that, should the
press be excluded from the courtroom.
French newspaper men would be treated
exactly the same as foreigners. The
meeting thereupon decided to treat the
Continued on Second Pago.
SAN MATEO IS TAKEN
BY AMERICAN TROOPS
Lively Fighting Within Ten Miles
of Manila, in Which Twenty-
Five Filipinos Are Killed.
, Special Dispatch to The Call.
MANILA, Aug. 13.â A recon
noisance yesterday by troops
of General B. M. Young's bri
gade, with the object of dis
covering the whereabouts of
the enemy near San Mateo, northwest
of the San Juan reservoir, about ten
miles from Manila, resulted in the
occupation of San Mateo.
The American loss was three killed
and thirteen wounded, including a
lieutenant of the Twenty-first Infan
try. Twenty-five of the enemy are
known to have been killed.
The Americans approached S;in Ma
teo in three nilumns. Major Cronin,
with fifteen men of the Twenty-fifth
Infantry, advanced from Novatiches,
five miles west of San Mateo. Cap
tain Rivers, with 100 men of the
Fourth Cavalry, and Captain Parker,
formerly lieutenant colonel of the
Twelfth New York Volunteer Regi
ment, with 2SO men of the Twenty
first and Twenty-fourth Infantry and
the Fourth Cavalry, .approached in
two columns from the south.
Major Cronin experienced many dif
ficulties arising from the condition of
the country and failed to effect a
junction with Captain Rivers, west of
San Mateo. as had been planned.

outpost of the enemy, two miles
BATES YET NEGOTIATES
WITH THE SULU SULTAN
' i Special Cable to The Call and the New York
â  Herald! Copyrighted, 1899, by James Gor
) don Bennett.
ILOILO. Philippine Islands. Aug. 13.â
J I have just returned to Iloilo after a
' l trip to Jolo, Mindanao and Cebu. Gen-
Meral Bates is still negotiating for a
' treaty with the Sultan of the Sulu
' Islands. The Sultan is as yet unwilling
;; to accept the terms offered by the
" '<â  Americans. He says that the Spaniards
) misrepresented to the world the nature
- ! of the treaty which he made with them,
) ; and he wishes to limit the Americans
to the occupation of the town of Jolo
i alone He claims the island of Siassi
and the town as his own. The Sultan
' '> seems not to have the support of his
-! chiefs, many of whom express friendly
i : sentiments toward the Americans. Yo
- kanine. the most powerful chief in the
- Sulu Islands, said in an Interview that
he was willing to force the Sultan to
i terms if he refused the American pr.op
l ositions. All th* chiefs complain of the
1 bad treatment the Sultan has given
I them. _.
. I The Chinese population oi Siassi,
I which was formerly held by the Span
, iards. but was given over by them to
' the Sultan-s men. are being squeezed
â¢ financially by a duty of 5 per cent on
! exports and imports. In an interview
â¢ the Chiese said it would be a most ex
-1 cellent change if the Americans were
1 substituted for their present rulers. The
1 : Sultan's Hag is now floating over
' Siassi The Sultan recently added
â  ! eighty rifles and a large store of am
' munition to his previous stock of three
hundred rifles.
1 A war between the controlling chiefs
1 ' among the Moros is more likely than is
a war with the Americans, provided we
do not interfere with their religion and
customs.
For the first time in its history white
people can travel about Sulu Island and
! among the Moros safely. 1 crossed the
island without a guard, through a wild
country to interview the Sultan at
; Maibun. He received me with courtecy
and gave me this signed statement:
"This certifies that his Highness Sul
tan Hadgi Mohamad Womolol Kiram.
is like a brother to the nation of Amer
icans and wants to know if they are
the same to him."
General Bates has shown great skill
in conciliating the native chiefs. Moro
chiefs of the island of Mindanao do not
! acknowledge allegiance to the Sultan of
Sulu. Dato, the chief mandi of Min
danao, came to visit Genera! Bates in
Jolo to assure him of his friendliness
toward the Americans.
On my trip to Mindanao T found the
'[ Moros friendly, but the towns of Zam
goango and Mercedes are held by insur
gents. They number 500 men and are
armed with rifles under their leader,
Alvarez, who stated to the commander
of the Castlne that he desired to be
neutral until the difficulty with Agui
naldo in Luzon was settled. If the lat
ter were beaten he would surrender.
In Tebu the insurgents still hold th<Mr
strong positions in th^ mountains. The
majority of the population do not want
to fight, but are hostile in spirit. A
native servant of an American officer
was caught by the insurgents and
! branded on the forehead with the word
"traitor."
In the case of Iloilo the insurgents
are intrenched about the nearby towns
Jaro and Molo. The rainy season pre
vents an active campaign, but consider
able fighting may be expected in the
fall. The rich natives have not returned
to their homes.
The island of Xegros is still unsettled.
General Hughes has sent extra troops
to suppress the uprising. The richer
class of natives are gradually return
ing to their homes.
TRICKY FILIPINOS
WITH FLAG OF TRUCE
MANILA. Aug. 14.â While the Seven
teenth Infantry, during last Tuesdays
battle, was approaching Oalulut along
the road, the troops saw a group of
fifty Filipinos outside the town under a
flag of truce. Some, who were in white.
clothing, held up their hands to signify
that they were unarmed.
Captain Hart, with a detachment, ad
vanced cautiously to a point within 200
yards of them, when the Filipinos
picked up their guns and fired a volley.
The Americans dropped into the bushes
unhurt on the first movement and re
turned the fire. At this the Filipinos
ran off.
Word has been received from Lieu
tenant J. C. Gilmore of the United
States gunboat Yorktown. who, with
fourteen members of the crew of the

by rlie- Americans, Captain Rivers
withdrew, covering his withdrawal
by a heavy volley. He lost a sergeant

INSURGENTS HAVE
TAKEN THE AGGRESSIVE
MANILA, Aup:. 13.â The insurgents
have taken the apprressive in the neigh
borhood of the railroad. On Saturday
nipht they unsuccessfully attacked San
Luis, on the Rio Grande, near Calum
pit, vhieh is garrisoned by two compa
nies of the Twenty-second Infantry.
The Americans had one man, a ser
geant, killed, and two privates wound
ed. Yesterday morning a similar affair
took place at Gringua, four miles west
of Malolos, where another small garri
son is stationed as a safeguard against
a possible attack upon the railway. A
special train took reinforcements to
Malolos and Guiganto, just north of
Bulacan.

RENNES, Aug. 13.â The battle has i

nre shown in the arrest of Paul Derou- j
lecie, the Deputy and poet, and twenty- i

great comma i
It is a strange thing how the fight \
thickened around the shadowy and j
emaciated red-haired Jew, whose uni- i
form of an artillery captain so ill fits J
and befits his figure and physiognomy. j
Last Monday's sitting of the court- |
martial and the subsequent non-public i

which France is divided and has been |

Each side stands committed to a cer

The actual Dreyfus trial is a trial of j

its ending in a drawn game. It is now
a "neck or nothing" combat, a desper
ate fight in which neither side will give I
in unless utterly routed.
The moral situation of the forces en
gaged reminds one of that of the armies
of the French revolution and those they
had to fight. The Dreyfusites view the
brains, the forward impulse, the dash
and the flexibility. At the same time
they are raw recruits gathered sponta
neously from the four points of the
compass. Hatred of the military caste
and may be the secret hoi of re
wards have made them homogeneous.
The Nationalists have social and
other prestige, numbers and organiza
tion. Their center is composed of the
majority of Frenchmen and French
women; their left wing is formed of 22,
--000 officers and their right wing con
sists of the church. All profess re
spect for legality, but only from the lips j
out. One side is just as unscrupulous
as the other, but the most active and
less numerous side does really fight for
the cause of humanity, justice and ]
truth. The Dreyfusites have, at any |
rate, won belligerent rights and they j
are sure of the friendly neutrality of
the Government.
Their adversaries can no longer make j
use of the rope that hung Pickard, or
the razor that cut Henry's throat. The j
Minister of War could not, if he wanted
to, read a forged document in parlia
ment. It is Impossible now to stuff the
dossier with fables n't for Mother Goose
tales. Rut organization, numbers, pres
tige and wealth mean staying powers.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, 31ONDAY, AUGUST 14, 1599.
THE SHERMAN'S COURSE FROM YOKOHAMA TO SAN FRANCISCO.
THE transport Sherman is now beginning to make an impression upon the miles of ocean that separate her from
San Francisco. At noon yesterday (San Francisco time) she had covered about 1365 nautical miles and should,
therefore be in the neighborhood of 3298 knots from this port. .
Cantain Barneson. superintendent of the transport service, said yÂ«iterday that he did not look for the
Sherman before the 25th inst. "She will average about 295 knots each twenty-four hours," said he, '^d reckon
ing that she left Yokohama at noon on the 9th she will not be very far from the Farallones at noon on the 25th mst.
J t' McMillan United States imutlcal expert, figures that at noon yesterday (San Francisco time) the Sherman
was in'latitude 39 degrees 40 minutci north, longitude 166 degrees 50 minutes east, and was, therefore. 3298 nautical
map showing the course of the transport is on exhibition in the window on the Market
street Bide of the business office of The Call. The map is attracting a great deal of attention, and crowds are gath
ered about the window to view it from early morning till late at night.

teenth annual convention of the Krei- j
gerbund and the twenty-tlfthannual re

of the Franco-Prussian war. The day j
signalized the opening of the conven- j
tions and the paying of tributes by
German-Americans to the men who
made possible the present greatness of
the < ferman empire.
The cordial relations between the
German-Americans and their fellow
countrymen was made manifest in the
enthusiastic cheers that greeted on
every hand the two young women rep- j
resenting Columbia and Germany, and;
in the singing, by a multiutde at Sharp
shooters' Park, of German and Ameri
can national anthems.
The day began with the arrival of
trains bearing delegations from all
parts of the country. As each train
slowed up at the depot committees
stepped forward to welcome the vis
itors. -Many of the arrivals were recog
nized by members of the committee as
former messmates on the battlefield,
and the welcome extended was a
hearty one. Among the visitors were
vereins from St. Louis, Cincinnati,
Cleveland. Elgin, Milwaukee, Dubuque
and many other cities.
To-morrow the business sessions of j
the conventions begin.

with timber on the streams. The North
ern Pacific Railway runs along the north
ern border, and the Burlington diagonally
Igh it from east to west.

true Roman; Gladstone was essentially an
Englishman, and Washington was tin- era.
Document nf the American spirit.
"But Jesus belongs in character to no
one nation or age. 11.- was manhood in
pi rfectlon. He had nor. the characteris
tics of a Jew. The world has crowned
Him as the idea! p< rfect man."
Two Homeless Children.
Two children, John and Joseph Gere,
I were lodged at the California-street Po
lice Station In a pitiful plight last night.
They had gone to their mother, who
spends her time in a. dive 011 Dupont
street, to pet the key to the woman's
room, at 127 Trenton street. She refused
to givp it to them. The luckless young
sters then searched for their father, find
ing him in a saloon. lie refused to have
anything to do with them. He had plenty
! of money to spend for drink, but none to
shelter his children.
Officers Fleming and Daggett took the
boys to the station. They will be doubt
less taken to some public institution to
day. Gere, who resides at 519 Jackson
street, has quarreled with his wife, and a
suit for divorce is pending between the
two.

ii" medicines. The Grant furnished the i

Open.
SEATTLE, Aug. 13.â recent seisure
of six Canadian fishing\boats near Point
Roberts by the United States customs
officials will probably be settled in a day

The matter ha^i been the subject of
diplomatic discussion and was finally re
ferred to United States District Attorney
Wilson R. Gay by Attorney General
Griggs. Mr. Gay reported that the seizure

in location, that It might be advisable to
release the boats. The Attorney General
wired him yesterday to proceed as he
thought best. Mr. Gay immediately
wrote Collector Iluestis, advising him to
let the boats go.

THE SAN FKANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, AUGUST 14, 1899.
RAILWAY FOR THE
REDWOOD COUNTRY
New Line in Mendocino
County.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
TIBURON, Aug. IS.â President A. W.
Foster of the California Northwestern
Railway Company dispatched a large
surveying force last week to make a
working location through the redwood
section northeast of T'kiah, in Mendocino
County, with a view to beginning the
extension of the line northward. This
move on the part of the company marks j
the starting point of a new era for
Mendocino and Humboldt counties anil
will be hailed with joy by all the resi
dents of that section of the State.
The pans- is under the direction of Civil
Engineei Harlow, a well-known railroad
surv >yor, and the work will be pushed
with all dispatch. It is hoped everything
will be in shape for the letting of a con
tract toward tin- close of the year.
If the original Intention of the company,
as expressed by Prealde.nl Foster at a
mass-meeting of the citizens at I'kiah five
years ago is adhered to, the road will he
extended as far northward as Eureka, the
â¢>- seat of Humboiiit County.
Not only will the farmers and he mer
chants of Potter Valley, Round Valley
and the towns and villages of Humboldt
County living close to the proposed line be
greatly benefited, but the extension
means the development of great natural
resources.
A few years ago a ledge of coal was
â  red near Round Valley, which in
vestigation by experts showed to be a
vein extending from a mine of enormous
extent. Analysis of this coal showed that
it was of B superior quality and a syndi
cate t" work the mine was formed. When
ii was found Impracticable to extend the
line of 'he San Francisco and North
Pacific Railway northward at that Lme
an attempt was made to form a company
to build a line from Westport, on the
roast, to the coal fields. This project had
to be abandoned on account of the great.
natural obstacles to be overcome. From
that day to this little has been done, but
interest will revive as soon as the rail
way ( ompany is found to be In earnest.
In addition to the coal fields, the pro
posed line will tap a region of redwood
forests that will furnish a practically In
exhaustible supply of lumber. Much of
this is of the "curly" variety, so valu
able In ornamenting the interior of
houses and in the manufacture of furni
ture. ___^________

MUTINY HELPED TO
DELAY THE WARREN
The United States Steamer Left All Her
Firemen in Jail in Manila,
Filipino Coal-Passersâ The Transport Was Twice
Caught in Typhoons and Was Anxiously
Looked for by the Centennial's Crew.
THE United States transport War
ren, now out fourteen days from
Yokohama with the First Colo
rado Volunteers aboard, has
been having a lively time of it.
Before she got away from Manila there
was a mutiny in the engine room and
twenty-four of the firemen and coal
passers are now serving sentences of
from thrÂ°e months to a year in Bilibid
jail. After getting away from Manila
with a Filipino crew in the fireroom,
she was twice caught in a typhoon and
was given up for lost at Nagasaki.
The Warren left Manila four days
after the Centennial. The day after
she sailed a typhoon raged far forty
eight hours and the Warren must have
got the full benefit cf it. The day after
rHE TRANSPORT WARREN, BEARiNG COLORADO TROOPS.
the Centennial arrived at Nagasaki an
other typhoon developed and detained
her several days in port. There were
no signs of the "Warren and a British
tramp which reached port could throw
no light on her delay. The tramp and
the Warren sailed from Manila to
gether, and the Britisher was caught in
both typhoons. The captain reported no
sign of the Warren after the first day
out.
Upon sailing from Nagasaki CaptF.ln
Eagles kept a bright lookout for the
Warren, but saw no sign of her, and
I in consequence felt very anxious until
| informed yesterday morning by the
pilot that the Warren sailed from Yo
kohama on August 1 for San Francisco.
The firemen on the Centennial say
that the trouble on the Warren was all
caused by the engineers. They worked
a sick man until he nearly dropped
dead, and then because the rest of the
men objected they were arrested.
"It was just this way," said a fire
man yesterday. "The engineers were
down on the white men because they
wanted Chinese or Filipinos. One of
the men named Edwards was sick and
asked to be excused from work. The
second assistant said he was shamming,
and told him to go on with his work.
Edwards tried to work, but couldn't.
Nevertheless he was forced into the
nreroom. and then the men began to
object. The entire force of firemen and
coal passers went before the chief and
complained of the way Edwards was
being treated. The chief ordered them
back to their work.
"Then there was trouble. Some of
the firemen went ashore and got drunk,
and when thew came back and found
Edwards trying to work in the flreroom
they were crazy. Then when the sick
man fell down in a faint they broke out.
The entire force of coal passers and

CONFERENCE NEARS AN END.
Bishop Games Will Depart for the
North To-Morrow.
The general conference of the African
Methodist Episcopal Church will reach
its close to-day. It will convene at 9:30
this morning and the appointments will
then be read by Bishop Games. who
leaves for Portland Tuesday. From Port
land the bishop will go to the Puget
Sound conference at Spokane.
The conference has been one of the most
successful and harmonious ever held on
the Pacific Coast, and its results will be
far reaching in the progress of the church.
Yesterday's services opened in the morn
ing with a sermon by Bishop Games on
the subject of "Sufficiency." The able
speaker talked convincingly on the quali
ties of a Christian minister. At the close
Of his sermon E. T. Hubbard of Mary's
ville, E. G. Lee of Fresno and Robert
Arrington of Redondo were ordained
elders. The services were solemn and im
pressive.
In the afternoon Rev. J. L. Whittier of
Riverside addressed the congregation In
the evening Rev. J. E. Edwards of Los
Angeles delivered an eloquent discourse
to a large congregation on the sub
ject of "The Indwelling Presence."
DEATH OF MRS. SUSAN A. HAM.
Her Illness Resulted From Being
Lost in the Mountains.
Mrs. Susan A. Ham died Saturday even
ing at frier home in .Alameda. after a long
illnpss resulting from a harrowing ordeal
through which she passed while camping
in the Santa Cruz Mountains last July.
Mrs. Hum was a woman of advanced
years. In July she joined her daughter,
Mrs. Abbie J. Forbes, who was spending
the summer near Boulder Creek. On the
morning of July It! Mrs. Ham left the
camp for a stroll through the hills. She
lost her way and remained in the mount
ains until the following Tuesday, when
nho was found by a rescuing party, lying
exhausted and unconscious beneath a
clump of underbrush. She was removed
to her home in Alameda, but never rallied
l'mm the shock until death relieved her
from her earthly sufferings. Deceased
was the widow of the late Charles W.
Ham and mother of Charles H. Ham of
the Polytechnic High School and Mrs.
Abbie J. Forbes. She was 78 years of age.

NORMAL SCHOOL
TERM WILL BEGIN
THIS MORNING
Seventy-Five Pupils
Are Accepted.
ONE HUNDRED IS THE LIMIT
THE TEST FOR ADMITTANCE IS
VERY SEVERE.
Model School Will Be Established in
Two Weeksâ Parents Are Re
quested to File Applica
tions Immediately.
The San Francisco State Normal School
will open its session for the year 18W-ISUO
this morning at 9 o'clock in the building
formerly occupied by the City Isormal
School on Powell street, between Sacra
mento and Clay.
Dr Frederic Burke, president of the
school, announces that seventy-five pu
pils have been accepted. One hundred in
all will be enrolled, and there are still
over one hundred to be examined. The
test has been made rather severe, and
one of the conditions exacted by Dr.
Burke is that only applicants who have
been recommended by the principal of the
school from which they have graduated
"ill be admitted. It is calculated that
by drawing from those recommended
uroswective teachers of a superior intelli
gencewil be turned out. The enforce
ment of this rule has caused many dis
appointments, but will raise the standard
Â° The a appH e cants for entrance are largely
from this city. though several come from
Berkeley. Oakland, Alameda, Merced,
Marysvlle and as far south as Fresno.
Three or four young men will take the
course Applicants eligible for admission
without examination are graduates of
Schools accredited by the State Univer
sity who present the credentials i whlcn
admit them to the University. No stu
dert will be admitted after August 21. The
coCrse of instruction will be two years
for graduation. The diploma of gradua
tion entitles the holder to a grammar
grade certilicate in any city or county in
the State of California.
The faculty will for the present consist
of George Leslie, who will preside over
the science department; Miss Agnes
Stoweil. teacher ot English branrnes;
MiÂ«s Estelle Carpenter, instructress in
music Professors Cubberley and Brown
will deliver lectures on Saturdays.
The Model School will be established m
two or three weeks, and parents are re
quested to file applications immediately.
This school is not preparatory to the
Normal School, but is a distinct branch,
where the corresponding studies in ihe
grades of the public school are taught
for the purpose of practical illustration in
the art of teaching by the pupils of the
Ncrmal School.
It affords a fine opportunity for pupils
who have failed in their school examina
tions to receive the necessary help in or
der to regain their places next year. The
classes will be limited to ten or ilttcun I
pupils, and more time can therefore be
devoted to their education. The tuition
fee has been placed at $2 a month. There
are twelve applications on file, and the
number of pupils will be limited to one
hundred. ___^__^____
FILIPINOS AS ACROBATS.
The Troupe Gives a Private Exhibi
tion at the Mechanics' Pavilion
Which Proves a Success.
"The Filipinos are away behind us in
the art of fighting," said Secretary Cum
ming of the Mechanics' lnstitute yesterday
afternoon, "but as acrobats, jugglers and
ring performers generally they will hold
their own with any nationality on earth."
The remarks of the secretary were
called forth by the results of a private
exhibition given by the Filipinos in the
Mechanics' Pavilion yesterday afternoon
to the directors of the institute and their
friends, which was voted a complete suc
cess. There are about forty performers,
and although they had not been able to
do much exercise since June last they
gave a most creditable exhibition when
they got loosened up in their joints, and
by steady practice they will without doubt
create a sensation. The directors and
friends were delighted with the exhibition,
and think they have struck a gold mine
as an attraction at the coming fair.
The programme yesterday afternoon
was opened by a selection from the Fili
pino band. They don't know a note of
music and play entirely by ear. They are
quick to memorize a tune, however, and
showed it by playing the familiar "Hot
Time in the Old Town" without a hitch.
Then the whole troupe, with the excep
tion of Tlster, who is still suffering from
the effects of carbon-monoxide, filed into
the ring and did some high and lofty
tumbling. "Tod Sloan," a little Filipino,
and another youngster made a hit and
will be a drawing card. A woman did
some clever feats on the wire rope, in
cluding undressing and dressing on the
rope. Then followpd exhibitions (in the
Roman rings, juggling, pole balancing,
pyramids of chairs, horizontal bars ami
concluding with a pantomime, "The Doc
tor and His Patient." introducing the
clown and Columbine. The first public
performance will bo given Friday.
A clock is being constructed for
LiverpoolrStreet station in London.
The interior of its case could allow five
persons to dine comfortably.
HITCHCOCK SCHOOL
(LATE SELBORNE),
SAN RAFAEL.
This school will reopen on August 29. with
handsome new buildings, heated by steam and
lighted by electricity. Every pupil has sepa-
rate room, choice of bedrooms on first and sec-
ond floors. Steam from outside, no furnace, no
stoves no flues in boys' quarters. The nearest
approach to a fireproof school. Arrangements
have been made foi boarding pupils and carry-
ing on the school in the Hotel Rafael until the
buildings are completed. For catalogues, testi-
monials and references apply to the principal,
REV. CHARLES HITCHCOCK. San Rafael.

BUSINESS STILL BRISK-
THERE is still no pause in trade worthy of note,
ugh there is a slight hesitation, as indicated i
by the hank clean: gs of the country, which
of 24.3 per cent last week, the smallest
increase for some time. There is, however, but little
g oil in the great cities, the gain at Chicago be
:ent, while all of the most important
- exhibit larger clearances than last year. New ;
York shows the most marked falling off, her gain last j
week being only 18.8 per cent over last year. De
creased trading in Wall street caused this decline, for;
doing very Httle speculation there at I
present, and the market has to depend on the regu
i, r professionals, most of whom are oft at the different
summer resorts.
kage in clearings is a shrinkage
in faili those! r the week being 136, against 196
ime week last ytar. Not for years have
mercial failures been so small and unimportant as
â  few months. This in itself is a signili
: of the remarkable prosperity of the coun
try.
Another very favorable indication is th<i volume of
â  crops, wheat ami corn. The Government
ts a yield of 500.000,000 bushels of win
ter and spring wheat, and a bumper crop of 2.001,
--500.000 bushels of corn, the latter being a record
breakeT. This cereal outlook of course affects the
great railroads, whose earnings during the coming
fail bid fair to show a marked increase. In fact,
1 ars are already becoming scarce in the East and
West, and the transportation companies will be put
to their trumps to move the vast output of the farms
of the West and Northwest.
The prominent staples keep' up their flattering ex
hibit, iron and steel of course leading. Western ad
are of heavy demand, scare* supplies of mate
rials, high price- and increasing volume of business I
i for next year. The production of pigirpn was
the largest ever known, but in spite of this stocks
showed a decrease and current production is easily at
rate of 14.000.000 tons- a year. Wool continues
.-'.rone, though as it is the season between clips the
market is not as active as it was some weeks ago.
Hides, leather, boots and shoes rule very firm, and
the manufacturers "have practically decided to advance
prices for the. litter. The demand for lumber shows
no abatement, and extensive canning operations have
greatly stimulated sugar. As for the canning trade
itself, it never was on a more brilliant footing. Most
of the California canners were sold up to their
capacity months ago, before the contracted fruit was
ripe, but of late a scarcity of cans has vexatiously
-retarded operations.
The local situation was enlivened toward the latter
part of the week by a rise in wheat, which though
fractional and on the very heels of a decline, was wel
come and made the street very cheerful. There is
nothing so stimulating to creneral trade as an advance
i-i wheat, and in an inverse ratio a decline in this
great staple always has a tendency to make merchants
cautious. No marked advance is expected, owing to
the favorable outlook for crops as a rule throughout
the world, but for some time the opinion has pre
vailed that wheat was > rather "lower than conditions
justified.
There is no slackening in the output of California
produce, which promises to be enormous this year.
The canners and driers are putting out immense quan
tities of fruit, which is meeting with an excellent de
mand at fine prices, owing to the shortage in the
and Europe. There is a stiff demand for new
hop?, but the growers will not sell, even at the good
prices bid by buyers. Cattle, sheep and hogs con
tinue high and wanted, provisions are in active de
mand, lumber is lively at the recent advances, a keen
inquiry for fall wool is expected by the trade, the
export trade of the coast was never livelier, and so it
goes all along the line, one unbroken exhibit of
prosperity.

DASTARDLY ATTACK ON T HE CITY.
T ptiE railroad company does not
want idle, desperate men running
around this city, and the citizens
of San Francisco do not want them.
1 â Extract jiom an address of the managing editor
\of the Examiner in reference to the returning
\ volunteers as reported vesterd.iv in the Examiner.
A writer tor the Examiner pleaded hysterically yes
| terday that somebody in California should do some
j thing "big"- in connection with the home-coming oi
\ the volunteers. The pleader asked for what he be-.
f lieved to be the impossible, something which would
, never be forgotten within the borders of the State
; and which would, in the memory of the nation, mark
California as the premier of States. The caslc of seem
ing impossibility has. in a single sentence, been ac
complished by the managing editor of the Examiner, j
This man has prostituted the patriotism of Americans
to gain a point in a petty quarrel for his employer.
He has outraged the sense of common decency
; which San Franciscans have always displayed. He has
; dishonored the State and has raised it to an evil
dignity of ill-fame because it harbors him and tolerates
the methods he represents. He has insulted the peo
ple of other States, and with a purpose baser than
that of him who barters his honor, has attempted to
brand the returning heroes of the Philippines as idle
I vagabonds, desperate characters, who come unwel
! come to the city and remain as feared and despised
malefactors.
With no other authority than his impudence, and
with no other criterion of decency than his own, he
speaks for the people of San Francisco and says that
; they do not want among them the brave men who
\ left their families and homes, their occupations and
'â  their ambitions to travel to the Southern seas to fight
for the nation's flag. It seems past belief that such
shameless effrontry could be shown even by the Ex
aminer, ready ever to betray a public interest and de
j base a private principle to serve its own selfish end.
but the proof is in the newspaper of which the man
aging editor is the authoritative head, and the motive
I is in the public's possession.
Not long ago the Examiner was deprived of its
; bribe money of $iooo a month by the Southern Pacific
! Company. The newspaper had become useless to de
! fend the corporation and harmless to injure it. The
! proprietor and his employe chafed under a sense of
| double injury. They felt as scoundrels whose scoun
drelism had been "made public. They have since lost
not a single opportunity to make reprisals. In tips
\ quarrel The Call is not concerned except where a pub
| lie interest is involved and where the people of thib
I city and State are to be made the victims of a
! dastardly misrepresentation which will do immeasur
! able harm.
When the managing editor of the Examiner says in
cowardly insinuation that the people of San Francisco
did not welcome the returning soldiers gladly he lies.
Tie speaks for himself and for his employer and in
their eyes is the vanishing glitter of a lost bribe.
When the managing editor of the Examiner declar-s
by blackguard implication that the citizens of this city
contributed their money and paid the tribute of their
praise to the volunteers in deceit he speaks the prin
ciple which has dominated his employer and the Ex
aminer in this community and he speaks for no one
else.
On Friday last the Examiner characterized the re
turning volunteers as worthy heroes. It suited the
Examiner then to call them so because a grudge
against General Shatter was to be satisfied. When a
bitterer spite was to be satisfied, when the double loss
of dishonest dollars and the sting of public shame were
to be revenged, worthy heroes became desperate, dan
gerous men.
But the Examiner has carried its despicable game
too far. It has made itself a public enemy. It has
attempted criminally, but in blind stupidity, to make
the people of the country and their returning heroes
the victims of its shameful scheme. In the face of
such an enemy the people of San Francisco have a
common cause. They are bound in duty to defend
themselves against an attack which would dishonor
their city in the view of all others and rob them of the
simple tribute of having done what their patriotism
dictated at a time when patriotism is the most sacred
virtue of a people who have given their dearest ones
as sacrifices to their nation's honor.
THE COMING AUTOMOBILE.
DISPATCHES from Washington announce that
the Government has been experimenting with
automobiles in the postoffice service and will
probably make extensive use of them in that city and
in Porto Rico. That is a curious combination. The
national capital and an outlying colony with a back
number civilization are to be made the scenes of
postal progress while the country at large is to be
kept waiting.
It would be impertinent, perhaps, to inquire why
the Government has decided to make its experiments
in Washington and a far-off island rather than in
San Francisco and Santa Clara Valley. There are
good reasons for the decision. Some time in the past
an automobile, under the highest patronage and with
the best auspices, started from New \ork for San
Francisco. The President and the Postmaster Gen
eral, as well as the rest of the country, have doubtless
been watching that experiment with close attention
and have learned from it so much that in undertak
ing experiments of their own they have thought it
best to begin operations on the smooth asphalt pave
ments of the capital or to try the country roads in a
remote island "far off amid the melancholy main."
The action of the Government will of course be
widely commended. It is well known the transconti
nental automobile is The Call's automobile, and the
people are always well pleased to see the Government
follow the leadership of The Call and profit by it.
Our automobile entered Ohio just before the
glorious primary elections of this city in which all the
bosses and the yellow journal were swept from' the
political horizon. In the uproar of that great event
the machine was lost sight of, but Ohio remains on
the map, and through the dawn's early light it can
be seen the machine is still there.
As there are women in Ohio with faces that have
been known to stop a freight train, some people may
suppose the automobile was stopped in that way.
Such a supposition would be erroneous. The self
propelling vehicle stopped in its own way. It is not
the first thing that has stuck on Ohio, and people
; shouldn't be too curious in such matters.
i In one respect the undertaking has been successful.
THE SAN FBJLBTCISCO CALL, 31OyMY, AUGUST 14, 1399.
It has verified the predictions of The Call. When the
automobile started The Call said the experiment
would test the mechanism and show where it is de
fective, and the results have been even so. There has
been revealed every defect in the machine from the
cylinders to the nut washers. It was said the trip
would test the country roads, and it has shown that
some roads are rough, some are mudoy and some
have ditches, and that the machine can break on the
rough" places, stick in the muddy places and run into
the ditches with the greatest facility. It was pointed
out that the appearance of the vehicle on rural roads
would prove whether people took an- interest in it and
whether cattle were scared of it, and so it did. It
was an object of more popular interest than a circus;
it was as startling to animals as a yellow journal freak
to humanity, and it once chased a cow for half a mile
and would have caught her if a valve hadn't broken.
â¢ The automobile is coming. It shall be in California
even as in Porto Rico. If the one now waiting for the
election processions in Ohio to pass do not reach
here in time for exhibition as a specimen of successful
mechanism at the Mechanics' Institute Fair, it will
still serve an exposition purpose. It will be hauled to
Chicago and placed. on a monument in front of the
Art Gallery of that city as the bust of the century.
OUR esteemed contemporary the Boston Her- i
aid devoted recently about a page of its space
to what is seemingly a tremendous sensation
in New England. The nature of the sensation was
well expressed in the headline: "Chinese Puzzle â Full j
Grown Celestials Pass as Boys â Find Fathers in Can- j
ada â Coolies Become -Merchants by a New Process â
The Exclusion Act Is Evaded â Revelations at Burl- I
ington, Vermont, During the Trials There."
From the vast array of facts spread forth over |
a page of closely printed matter, it is learned the bland
ainl child-like coolie has matched his unsophisticated ;
( (riental ingenuity against the shrewdness of the
Yankee in the Yankee's own home and has got the
better of him.
From the evidence given, it is clear there can be
no telling how many Chinese have managed to get i
into this country by the back door of New England. :
It is known now that hundreds who had no right to '
enter have not taken the trouble to smuggle them
selves in, but have walked up to the officers of the
Government and cleverly passed themselves off as i
American-born or as persons privileged under the
treaty.
It is noted that the cases most difficult to deal with
by the New England authorities are those of Chinese
claiming to have been born in the United States,
but educated in China. The thing that puzzles^ Boston I
is the fact that these natives have no difficulty in find
ing fathers in New England to vouch for them a-; his
children, pnd sometimes the father does not look
much older than the boy. Moreover, the number <<f
the natives is larger than seems quite right to New
England calculators. ;
The Herald says:
"It is noticed that the most of the boys coming j
in claim to have been born in San Francisco between ;
[875 and 1880. They do this because at that time there
was no adequate registry of births of Chinese in that
city. There could not have been more than 200
Chinese wives in that city during the years specified.
A birth rate of 50 boys a year would have been
abnormally large; yet in Vermont alone, for the five
years preceding this one, from 700 to 1000 alleged
native-born Chinese have been admitted, and the
number in the country is enormous."
The merchant dodge also is worked a way that
fatigues the Boston intellect. It is said:
"In Boston there are about 200 Chinese merchant*. !
and yet during 1897 and 1898 there were filed 3500
names of Chinamen alleged to be in business in Bos
ton. The method is this: A firm files a list of thirty
five partner?, and in the course of two years twenty
five will be admitted as returning partners. Logically
there should now be sixty partners, but as a matte r
of fact there are still thirty-five, but the names are all
new ones. When asked where the old partners have
gone, the reply is that they have sold oat and their
whereabouts is unknown. These new names remain
until they have served the purpose of unlawfully in
ducting some more Chinamen into the country, and
so the endless chain goes on."
Altogether the ingenuity of the little yellow man is
too much for the philanthropists. Even when they
know his tricks they cannot head him off. They
may know him to be lying when he claims to be i
native son of California or a merchant of Boston, but
they cannot catch him at it. He has his witnesses,
his certificate?, and everything else the officers de
mand, and it appears that ere long the New England
schoolteacher will not have to come to San Francisco
to see a Chinatown.
\A OST excellent is tlie suggestion of a cor
/ \ respondent that the programme of the theatri
* cal entertainment to be given for the benefit
of the volunteers' reception fund, be repeated at the
Grand Opera House. The plan has a double attrac
tion inasmuch as it will not only increase the revenue
to be derived from the entertainment, but will enable
persons who are not rich enough to compete in the
bidding for seats at the Orpheum to have a share in
the dramatic festival.
The details of the plan are given in our cor
respondent's letter published elsewhere, and need not
be repeated here. Suffice it to say the project is thor
oughly feasible. Mr. Morosco has offered the use of
the Grand Opera House, so there will be no difficulty
in that direction. For the actors and actresses to pass
from the one place to the other will be easy. Many
of them in the course of their careers have doubtless
made such double plays more than once, and it will
not be too much to ask them to repeat the perform
ance for the patriotic object in view.
It must be borne in mind the reception of the volun
teers is a movement in which all classes of citizens are
interested. Those who cannot afford to pay $50 or
even $5 for a seat at the benefit are just as patriotic
as those who can. State pride, national patriotism,
loyalty to the heroes of war and joy in their return
with victory, are feelings shared by all classes of citi
zens. It is right therefore that all should have a
chance to share in every undertaking set on foot for
the purpose of raising money for the reception.
Our correspondent is to be thanked for his sugges
tion. It is timely, pertinent, patriotic and popular.
It should be at once adopted and acted upon.
Mayor Phelan's position on the new tax levy seems
to be pretty well defined. He will stand sponsor for
all in it that is good and pass the buck to the Super
visors for the rest of it.
The authorities at Washington are now begging for
bread for the people for whom only a short time ago
there was a demand for bullets.
The Dominican insurgents are trying to hire Cuban
soldiers. There are some commodities not worth ac
cepting without cost much less buying.

STATE MINING LAW.
MINERAL DISCOVERIES.
The news that because of a blunder in I
â the construction of the repealing act of
March 20, ISSQ; the State mining law of
I 1597 stands unrepealed will give the miners
and prospectors the biggest shaking up i
they .have received in a long time.
That the Legislature did not accom- ,
[â¢lish the repeal of the act of March 27,
is: 7. was early suspected or believed by
j a number of mining lawyers and mining
men, and several mining attorneys have
advised people to proceed under the State
law in making locations, but the discov
; cry did not become generally discussed or
, known, and miners proceeded according to
, old laws and forms all over the State, and
â  the announcement of the remarkable sit
uation in The Call of Saturday was prac
tically the first notice of their trouble the
, miners received.
After the adjournment of the Legisla
i ture 'the miners were much at sea re
garding the condition of the mining laws,
and the leading authorities appealed to
; â by hundreds of inquirers kept repeating
j that the situation was simply as it was
j before. the State law was passed, mining
j .locations being governed wholly by the
Federal, law and by district regulations,
where such had been made. Late In May
; last Attorney General Ford wrote an opin
i ion on the . situation' from this standpoint,
! which was published in The Call of May
28', and added the following:
"Note â appears to be a slight er
ror in the wording of the repealing act of
, 1599, the effect of which I have not con- |
i sidered. I have assumed in the foregoing
; that the act of 1897 was effectually re-
I pealed."
This was the first published intimation
that anything was wrong with 'the repeal
ing act, which few took the trouble to look
up, but it went" unheeded and the State
law was disregarded. it was on the as
â  sumption that the repeal had been effect
; ed that the executive committee of the
1 California Miners' Association a few
weeks ago decided to have prepared forms
' of location notices according to the Fed
eral law, for which there was much in
quiry by miners, and it was during the
preparation of these forms by Attorney
: A. H. Ricketts last week that the discov
ery which became effective was made.
After thorough study Mr. Ricketts decided
that there was no question about the
i State law being still in force, and other
eminent attorneys agreed with him. Three
or four days ago E. H. Benjamin, secre
tary of the California Miners' Associa
tion, sent notice of this conclusion to va
rious people, and then The Call made it
generally known.
As stated in The Call on Saturday, the
act appears in the statutes as follows:
Charter CXIII.â An act to repeal "An act
j prescribing the manner of locating mining
' claims upon the public domain of the United
i States, recording notices of local thereof,
amending defective locations and providing for
I the deposit of district records with County Re
i corders, and prescribing the effect to be given
to reoordaxion of notices of location and affi
davits," approved March 27. 18S7.
1 The people of the .State of California, repre
| sented in Senate and Assembly, do enact as
I I'olluws,:
Section Iâ An act to repeal "An act prescrib
, ing the manner of locating mining claims upon
the public domain of the United States, record
ing notices of location thereof, amending de
fective locations and providing for the deposit
of district records with County Recorders, and
prescribing the effect to be given to recordation
; of notices of location and affidavits," approved
March 27. ls'.-T, is hereby repealed.
Sec. 2â This act mi. til take effect immediately.
The title of the act is all right, but a
; moment's consideration of the construe
! tion of Section l reveals the error. The
trouble can be quickly understood by
omitting the matter in quotation marks
I and reading: "An act to repeal (an act,
etc.), approved March 27, iv<7. is hereby
repealed. This accomplishes nothing. It
! repeals "an act to repeal," and there was
Ino such act to be repealed. If the act
I had read: "An act entitled 'An act, etc.,
â¦ â¢ â¢' is hereby repealed," the end sought
would have been accomplish* d.
Secretary of State Curry has certified
to the Mining and Scientific Press that the
act as published in the statutes is a true
I copy of the original bill. The legal opin
-1 ions of Mr. Kick, and others to the
effect that as Section 1 expresses a precise
and clear meaning in saying "an act to
repeal, etc.", is hereby repealed," a court
cannot assume an intent different from
that so expressed, and cannot substitute
the intent expressed in the title for that
expressed in the act itself. The intent of
the Legislature is not for judicial in
quiry. When there Is no ambiguity the
error cannot be cured by a court, and the
Stale mining law was never repealed.
Attorney General Ford naturally de
clines to commit himself too definitely in
advance of official action, but was will
ing to say: "The repeal of the law of 1897
is extremely doubtful, and I strongly ad
vise miners to strictly observe the State
law."
If the State law has been continuously
In effect since March 27, 1597, no mining
location made since then otherwise than
in accordance with it is a valid one. If |
It is not a valid location legal possession j
cannot be maintained, and the claim is !
subject to relocation by any one. Very
few claims have been located according
to that law since the supposed repeal,
and with claims located less than sixty
days before the attempted repeal but few
holders properly completed the location
by tiling a second notice, an, affidavit of
$50 worth of work, etc.
Hundreds of miners and holders or
claims by bond or purchase will be In sad
predicaments, and there will be a great
amount of trouble and uncertainty for
a long time. There has been more pros
pecting and more claims taken up this
year than ever before. On one day re
cently fifty location notices were Hied
with the recorder of Shasta County.
Prospectors have this year penetrated in
great numbers into every part of the min
eral regions of Northern California and
swarmed along the Sierra Nevadas and
over the southern districts. The copper
boom has resulted in the location of a
large number of copper claims from Fres
no County north through .ill the north
ern counties. A large number of oil
claims help swell the list. Practically
all these claims located during the past
six months will be without legal protec
tion until the State law is complied with.
As under the State law $50 worth of work
is required within sixty days, it will be
impossible for the average holder of a
number of claims to save them all by
promptly relocating them, the tax on his
resources being too great. With the sup
posed disappearance of this requirement
many prospectors proceeded to stake out
claims as fast as they could find anything
to locate, having, they assumed, un
till the end of the year 1900 to do the $100
worth of assessment work required by
the Federal law. With the return of the
$."0 and sixty-day requirement a great
many of these claims will be simply aban
doned. Some miners are far from claims
they have located, and some will not learn
poon that they must hustle if they have
claims they want to save. It is to be re
gretted that the way seems legally open
for claim-jumping and the troubles that
would follow, for a claim cannot be held
unless properly located, and the State
law now discovered to be in force saya:
All locations of quartz or placer formations
or deposits hereafter made which do not con
form to the requirements of this act. in so far
as the same are respectively applicable there
to, shall be void. .
The only thing for claim holders to do
is to promptly relocate according to the
State law and do their assessment wo-k
within two months. The forms of notices
required by it must be again resorted to.
There will be a season of much doubt and
inquiry and a variety of complications
will arise all over the State. '.!.". %

Another discovery â¢of molybdenite in I
this State has been made, this time in
Ventura County. The following account, j
accompanied by samples of quartz rock
pronounced at the Minings-Bureau quite
rich in molybdenite, has been received by
The Call: â  .â¢ - -.Â»
L.EBEC, Kern County, Cal., Auk 7. 1899.
Editor Cad-Sir: Molybdenite has been dis
covered in the Gold Hill country. \entimi ,
County. For two or three years past the nun
ers and prospectors of this section nave been
troubled with a mineral supposed to be graph- j
ite, which does not occur in sufficiently large |
quantities to be of commercial value, ajia ow-
Ing to Its rebellious nature was considered a
nuisance. Investigation, however, has dis- i
closed the fact that the supposed graphite is
molybdenum. James McDonald, a miner who ;
knows mineral when he sees It. recently dis
covered such large . deposits of this metal in a
prospect he was opening up for gold on the
Alamo Mountain that he determined to ascer
tain Its true character and sent samples to a
Los Angeles metallurgist for analysis, with the
result stated. The report also shows a per
centage high enough to warrant a systematic
and thorough development of the ore body.
The mineral occurs in oxide, sulphide and car
bonate forms In a vein of Iron-stained quartz
six feet wide, inclosed between gray granite
walls, and has been traced about three miles j
along the line of strike. V. E. Fi Â«TS
Molybdenite is the chief ore of molyb
denum which has been classed as one of I
the "rare metals," though its occurrences j
are numerous. It has been found In many
places through the Eastern States and
in the western mineral regions. Including
California. It has been reported several,
times from Fresno and Kern .counties, I
and has been found elsewhere and has i
several times excited the interest and in- ;
quiry of prospectors who thought they i
had made valuable finds. It is found in ;
quartz granite and other rocks, generally j
in thin, irregular layers, and composing
but a very slight percentage of the rock.
It is easily mistaken for graphite, being
similarly soft and producing a similar
mark when rubbed on the lingers, but it i
may be distinguished by its bluish gray
cast, while graphite is black. Malybde- j
num is chiefly used in hardening and
toughening steel, for which purpose it is
substituted for tungsten, and for making i
a blue coloring for porcelain. So small
a quantity is demanded that the world is
practically supplied by one mine in Swe
den at about 90 cents per pound. It Is
best obtained from the ore in an electric
furnace. The supply is abundant -when
ever it is demanded. The Ventura j
County discovery would thus appear |
more interesting than commercially im- ;
portant. In auriferous quartz, malyb
denite interferes with amalgamation and
would hence be troublesome in working
the ore for gold.
California's first talc mine has been
opened in Ventura County by S. S. Simon
and talc is for the first time being pro
duced commercially in this State. Talc
is found abundantly all through the State
but rarely of sufficient whiteness and
purity to be of any commercial value. It
is largely demanded for the manufacture
of paper and for other uses, and It is ex
tensively produced in the East.
' J. O. DENNY.
PACIFIC COAST SURVEY.
Superintendent of the Service
Tells of the Work Done.
The main work of the survey consists
in the preparation of coast charts. At
present charts of California, Oregon and
Washington coasts have been carried to
a good deal of accuracy, but In Alaska
the survey is meeting the most difficult
problem it has yet to face in the 100 years
of its history. In Alaska are 20,00") miles
of uncharted coast. Navigation is still
further hindered by fogs. Until the last
five years but little need had been felt
for accurate charts of this region, but
within that time an enormous commerce
PROFESSOR H. S. PRITCHETT.
has sprung up in Alaskan waters and
every effort is being made to survey the
harbors and bays of the Alaskan coast
line. During the present season the sur
vey has five vessels at work in Alaskan
waters. Three of these are at work in
the neighborhood of the Yukon delta.
Into which stream an entrance from the
ocean Is being sought. Surveys of Cape
Nome and Golovim Bay are also being
made. Another party is at work in
Prince William Sound, and during the
paÃt season the territory about the head
of the Lynn Canal, and which Is in
volved in* the boundary dispute, has been
surveyed and the information furnished
to the Department of State. At present
the most pressing need for surveys la
felt in regard to Prince William Sound
and Cooks Inlet and the passes through
the Aleutian Islands.
During the coming winter the survey
of the coast of Hawaii will be begun.
Congress has also authorized the begin
ning of surveys in the Philippines, but
this work will doubtless waft the pacifica
tion and gradual development of the is
lands.
Besides these great general lines of
work which the survey is pushing, the
following pieces of work are of special
interest to San Francisco and California:
1. The erection of a tidal indicator on
Alcatraz Island.
An automatic tidal indicator is in pro
cess of construction which will be erected
on Alcatraz Island within a few months.
This apparatus, by means of a large disk
over which an arm moves, will show to
the shipping in the harbor the exact
height of the tide and also whether it is
rising or falling, information of the
utmost value to the shipping.
2. The erection of an observatory at
T'kinh for determination of the variation
of latitude.
This work is carried on by the Coast
Survey for the International Geodetic As
sociation. This association was formed
a few years ago by about twenty of the
leading nations of the earth for a co
operative study of the size and figure of
the earth and of other geodetic prob
lems. Among these problems, and onÂ»
very recently brought to the attention of
geometers, is the phenomenon known as
the variation of latitude. It is found that
thin variation Is due to th* fact that the
earth's axis instead of being fixed a* the
earth travels through â pace, has a Flight
wobble bo to apeak. This displacement
may throw the, earth's axis as much a*
fifty feel away from the plane it would
occupy if fixed, and consequently change
the latitude by this amount. In order to
completely determine the amount of thlr
chTnce and Its period the Internationa^
association has established six obserya
rorlea in exactly the same Parallel of
latitude Two of these are in the United

NEWS OF FOREIGN NAVIES.
Coaling at sea receives much attention,
in foreign navies. The international
squadron at Crete had a competitive test
In which the British came out aw;->
--nhead. The French took in thirty to forty
tone an hour, the Italians twenty tons, the
Germans, Austrians and Russians aver
aged about ten tons, while the British
took in their coal at a little over a hun
dred tons per hour.
At the recent meeting of the Institution
of Naval Architects in London, a paper
was read from Chief Engineer Melvilbi
relating to the triple-screw system and
strongly advocating its extension. In
discussing the paper read Sir White, direc
tor of naval construction in the British
navy, declined to enter into any critical
analysis of the unreliable proposition,
saying that he was anxious to keep an
open mind on all subjects, but that Mr.
Melville's paper contained but a few
facts, a great many opinions, not a few
speculations and some arguments."
The French Ministry of Marine finds it
self in a predicament such as is unparal
leled in any other navy. The sea-groin?
officers are averse to shore duty and its
attendant sedentary occupations, and
prefer to be either unemployed or to go to
.st-a. As a consequence orders have twen
issued that hereafter officers must taka
their turn of shore duty for a certain un
interrupted period, and no excuse will be
accepted to escape office work in the sev
eral departments or at yards. There is no
such objection in our navy against shore
duty, and such officers as have declined
duty at Washington are phenomena.
With the new scale of pay there is. on
the contrary, likely to be a scramble for
shore duty, as the pay in some grades is
now actually less white at tea than on
shore. No such inducement exists In ir.y
other navy to forego sea service and ac
cept the monotony of three years" shore
duty.

The Ceremonies atthe

AN IMMENSE THRONG ATTENDS
AN ABLE SERMON BY BISHOP
MONTGOMERY.
Beautiful Music Rendered by an Aug
mented Choir â Kind Words for
Pastor and Congrega
tion.
His Grace, the Most Rev. Archbishop
TUordan, In the name of God yesterday
received the handsome edifice erected for
the Holy Cross Church from the hands of
the faithful by whose piety and generos
ity it was built, and solemnly dedicated it
to the exclusive purposes of divine wor
ship. Henceforth the structure is with
drawn from profane use and, in the em-
Iphatic- language of the Scriptures. It be
comes a "house of God."
The ceremonies attending the dedication
â¢ â â¢: Holy Cross 1 new home were impressive
and beautiful. There was not a person
within the walls of the sacred edifice who
was not awed by the solemnity of the
occasion. The Archbishop, robed In full
canonicals and attended by deacons of
honor, was present, as were also the
bishops of the northern and souther] sec
tions of the State, tog-ether with many
priests from the various parishes in this
City and vicinity. Acolytes attired in red
cassocks to the number of twenty par
ticipated in the services, and their cos
tumes, in contrast with the austere garb
of the priests, had a striking effect.
Had the church been twice its size it
could hardly have held the crowd which
eagerly tried to gain admittance. With
wise forethought the pastor and his as
sistants issued cards of admission to the
members of the parish, and these magic
boards opened the way to a seat.
Those unable to gain admittance content
ed themselves with standing in the vesti
bule for hours.
The services opened promptly at 10:30
a. m. with an organ prelude. The
Archbishop, preceded by the acolytes
bearing lighted candles, officials of the !
mass and the visiting priests, entered the !
chancel and began the ceremony of dedi-
I cation Accompanied by the officiating
clergymen his Grace first proceeded down j
the center aisle to the front of the church, ;
and as those In the procession gathered !
around him he sprinkled holy water on j
the doors and walls, accompanying the
action with appropriate prayers.
On returning to the altar the litany was
chanted, and again the. procession moved ;
with solemn tread through the aisles of !
the church, the Archbishop sprinkling the ,
walls of the church while the deacons of .
honor alternately read prayers from the
service of dedication. On the return to ;
the chancel the celebration of the mass ;
commenced. Rev. E. A. Kelly of Chicago, ,
a classmate of Father John F. McGinty, j
was the celebrant of the mass; Rev. John j
E. Cottle of St. Brigid's, deacon; Rev. ,
John P. Coyle of the Star of the Sea, sub- :
deacon; Rev. P. E. Mulligan, master of i
ceremonies, and Rev. R. Sampson, assist
ant master of ceremonies. Gounod's |
"Grand Messe Solenelle" was rendered by
the choir during the mass. At the con- !
elusion A)f the solemn high mass Father '
McGinty made a few announcements, and
was followed by Rt. Rev. Bishop Mont
gomery, who preached In his usual elo- !
quent style. He spoke in part as follows:
And Jesus told to them, go, therefore,
into th- whole world and teach the gospel
to every creature. He that belleveth and
Is baptized . hall be saved; he that be
lieveth not shall be condemned. Having
piven the text he addressed himself to the
Archbishop, Bishop, reverend gentlemen
and the brethren as follows: '"It Is my
happy privilege to-day to congratulate the
pastor and the people of Holy Cross parish
on the completion of this magnificent
church and Its solemn dedication to the
service of Almighty God. Henceforth it â 
will stand as a monument of the faith, the
zeal and the piety of both pastor and peo
ple. Whilst is a true that the dedication of
a house of worship in an active and grow
ing population la not a matter of rare
occurrence, nevertheless it always brings
to our mind the mission and the work of
the church as an institution. We learn
when very young in our catechisms that the
â work of the church Is to teach, sanctify and
save souls. The work of the church ie ever
the same. Her object Is to teach the truths
of God and apply them to the souls of men
and pave them."
The speaker then told of the obstacles, dif
ficulties, and temptations in the way of such
teaching.
At the offertory "O Cor Amoris" was
rendered by Miss Lilly Roeder and Miss >
Ella V McCloskyn. The Archbishop con
cluded the services by giving the pontifi- i
cal blessing. While standing on his throne
he delivered the following words to the
congregation:
I would like to pay one word before we
leave this beautiful church. I desire to
extend first my congratulations to the rev
erend pastor of the parish and then to his
devoted parishioners my heartfelt congratu
lations on the completion of this great
work begun but a few months ago for the
honor and glory of God, and to-day dedi
cated to divine worship.
It is not an â  easy matter to build a
church like this. I can speak from ex
perience. The nights of anxiety, the days
of labor, the going about securing aid is
not congenial, and yet your pastor has done
it successfully and quickly, and has done
It because you were impressed with the
need of a church. And to him first of all
congratulation and to you who aided him
my heartfelt congratulations.
The Archbishop was attended by i
fathers Lagan and Frieden as deacons of '
honor. Bishop Montgomery had Fathers
Vlebert and Sardi as deacons of honor, j
and Bishop Grace of Sacramento, Fathers ;
Pius Murphy and D. O. Crowley. Among
the priests in the sanctuary were: Fathers i
Dempsey, Cummlnes. Culligan, Santan- j

F. J. Deford, a dishwasher, was booked

on a charge of grand larceny. About 4
o'clock the officers were notified that a

street, and they saw Deford walking

on the sidewalk. They arrested D<
Whfr searched nothing was found in his

Spitz, a cigarmaker. living at 638 Minna
street, and he said he had lost his watch
and chain. Spitz was sent to the Receiv

Blood Poisoning Causes Death.
M. M. McGregor, a farmer residing
near Modesto, died at the French Hos
pital in this* city Saturday evening from
blood poisoning. McGregor was caught in
a thrashing machine and had his ri; m
hand badly lacerated. He came to this
city a few days ago for medical treat
ment, and the doctors at the hospital
told him that it would be necessary to
amputate the member in order t<> pre
serve his life. To this proposition Mc-
Gregor objected and soon the fatal blood
poisoning set in.

THE SAX FRAK CISCO CALL, MONDAY. AUGUST 14, 1899.
THE TRANSPORT
CENTENNIAL IN
GREAT DANCER
Went on a Coral Reef
Near Manila.
CARGO HAD TO BE JETTISONED
FILIPINOS TRIED TO CAPTURE
THE STEAMER.
One of the Centennial's Crew Killed.
Sick and Wounded Soldiers
Came Home on the
Transport.
The Tnited States transport Centennial
arrived from Manila yesterday morning
after a most exciting round trip. S â¢
I narrowly escaped a couple 6f typhoons,
was ashore on a coral reef and the en
tin crew barelj escaped capture at the
hands of the Filipinos; one of the sailors
siiled by a parting hawser and one
of thf quartermasters was stabbed by a
colored cook. In spite of all these inci
both officers and men are well sat
isfied with their ship, and all of them are
eager to go out on her again when she
sails for the Philippines.
The Centennial left here in May last
with a valuable cargo of supplies for Gen- i
oral Otis. While rounding the north end
of the Island of Luzon she struck on a
Bunken reef off Point Engano and stuck
there hard and fast for fourteen hours.
Filipinos in bancas and canoes put off
from the mainland and hovered around
the steamer. Some of them ranged along- i
side, but Captain Eagles ordered them '
away, and when some of his men ap- '
I armed with rifles the Filipinos
Sheered Off.
Soon after this a hand of mounted rebels
arrived on the beach. They sent word to
Captain Eagles that in the morning they
would bring a thousand men from the In
terior to help to get the vessel off. AH
this time the engines of the Centennial
had been going full speed astern, but they
failed to budge the transport. Captain
Eagles, seeing that if hr- waited for high
water an armed band of Filipinos would
swoop down on him. began to jettison the
cargo. Machinery, saddles and bridles,
canned beef, cases of hardtack, canned
fruit, salmon and other supplies were
dropped overboard. In all, goods valued
at over $13,000 were thrown out. and the
pile was almost out of the water when the
ship floated oft' the ledge.
"We were all badly scared, I can tell
you," said one of the officers yesterday.
"Captain Eaglea told us that if we did
not get the vessel off the Filipinos would
Joot the cargo and every one of us would
be made prisoners or killed. While we
were hoisting the cargo over the side the
Filipinos increased in number and became
more and more venturesome, as they
thought we would be unable to get off.
Tlkti- were Indications of their not wait
ing for the help that was coming from
the interior, and once or twice I made
sure they were going to board us. Finally
Second Assistant Engineer Gottlob and
Third Assistant Carr got a kedge out over
the stern and we managed to warp the
Centennial out Into deep water. We were
none too soon, either, i can tell you, for
just as we got the steamer under way
the 'promised assistance 1 arrived. It con
sisted of about a thousand armed rebels,
and it would have been all day with us
if we had still been on that coral reef."
The Centennial reached Manila without
any mishap after that. On her arrival
divers were Bent down, who reported that
part of the keel was gone but the vessel
was not otherwise damaged. She will pro
on the drydock here for an overhauling,
and before sailing again for the Philip
pines will have new boilers put In.
Charles Jackson, one of the crew of the
Centennial, was brought here in irons.
lie is charged with stabbing Quartermas
ter Herman Brevy. From what the men
on the transport say Brevy was the ag
gressor. He called Jackson names and be
pan to punch him. Jackson then pulled a
I sheath-knife and plunged It into ISrevy's
abdomen. The wounded man was taken
; to St. Bernard's Hospital, at Nagasaki,
where he lay in a precarious condition
when the Centennial sailed. On the ar
rival of the transport Jackson was handed
over to the harbor police, who will turn
him over to the United States Marshal to
i day.
One of the Centennial's crev* was killed
| at Manila by a parting hawser, and a sol
' dier was nearly swept overboard. There
was a terrific strain on the cable as the
' ship was swinging to it. Captain Eagles
warned the soldier and Quartermaster
Hanson to seek a place of safety. Neither
man heeded the warning, and just as the
rope slipped out of the "chock" the mate
grabbed the soldier and saved him. The
sailor was struck by the rope, and his
arms and legs were broken. He also re
ceived internal injuries from which he
died In a couple of hours. The Centennial
] brought up the following passengers:

Overton is wounded in the leg. Dr. Kemp
has been ordered to Fort Hamilton, New
York.
The Centennial was thirty-two days
coming from Manila, and twenty-one days
from Nagasaki. She sailed from Manila
just after a typhoon had been raging, and
reached Nagasaki immediately before one
broke out. The transport behaved well
throughout the trip, and her officers all
sing her praises. They say that any other
I steamer would have been pounded to
pieces on the coral reef at Point Engano,
while the Centennial escaped with a few
dented plates and the loss of a portion
of her keel. __
RED MEN CHIEFS ARRIVE.
Escorted From the Ferry to Turners
Hall by Local Braves.
The pram! officers and delegates of the
Independent Order of Red Men arrived
from the East at quarter-past 10 o'clock
yesterday morning. The local lodge, fa.
dfic No. 66, turned out 500 strong to
meet them, ar>out fifty of the t cort be
ing mounted and attired in the full war
regalia of the American Indian.
Twenty-five carriages completed the
m ssion. containing, besides the
visitors, sixteen of the officers and ex
presldents of the Daughters of Poca
hontas.
At Turner Hall an ample breakfast
was served, Grand Chief Fowhattan
Ackermann. Grand Chief of California
Landmann, C. H Rode and others making
short ad'lross^p. After the banquet me
grand officers wrere conducted to the
Golden West Hot< !
To-day will be spent in sightseeing, the
Supreme Lodge convening Tuesday
morning. Among the visiting officers are:
Grand Supreme Secretary Gustave AVahle
of Chicago and P. Lenderking, grand
supreme treasurer, of Baltimore.
AT THE PARK AND CLIFF.
Crowds Were Small Owing to the
Threatening Weather.
The threatening weather kept many
people indoors y< sterday, and as a natural
result the crowd- at the park, beach and
other pleasure places were small. No ac
cidents of serious nature occurred, which
was fortunate, as the Park Emergency
Hospital still remains closed. At the
Chutes many people enjoyed the various
: attractions," Interest being divided oe
tween the zoo and the vaudeville perform
| ance in the pavilion. At the Sutro Baths

Watsonville Victorious.
WATSON VI I-LiE, Aug. 13.â 1n the pres
ence of one of the largest crowds at Por
ter's Park this season the Watsonville
baseball club defeated the Santa Cruz
team this afternoon by a score of 8 to 3.
The visiting club was cheered on by a
large delegation of Santa Cruz fans, but
their efforts were futile against the bril
liant pitching of George Harper and the
excellent team work of. the Watsonvillans.
There were times when it locked as if
Santa Cruz would fatten its score, but
the cleverness of Harper and the sharp
work of the fielders cut off the chances
for hit:- and runs. Ed Daubenbis was in
the box for the Beachcombers, and though
he was found often he was ; not entirely
sponsible for the loss of the game. Score:

ONE MORE RACE
FOR COLUMBIA
AND DEFENDER
Will Compete for the
Astor Cups.
CONTEST EXCITES INTEREST
NEW YACHT EXPECTED TO WIN
EASILY.
Should It Defeat Its Competitor by
Five or Six Minutes the Ix
perts Will Bs
Satisfied.
Special DWpaten to The Call.
NEWPORT, R. 1., Augr. 13.â 1f it were
â not for the fact that both the Columbia
ami the Defender have entered the race
to-mprrow for the Astor cups the events
would be almost featureless, for to-night
out of a net of over sixty sailing craftâ
sl.xjps and schoonersâ only six- yachts.
three .:f each, have signified their inten
tion to enter the race. Still the race' will
be watched by a great crowd of yachts
men, and will undoubtedly be as interest
ing as those in years gone by. A victory
(or th> Columbia is on the card, and over
a. course of thirty-eight miles a lead of
five or six minutes would satisfy every I
one. The contest called this year for the
second time the Astor cup race, was in
stituted many years ago by Ugden Goelet,
who offered two cv**Â« one. for schooners
and another {of sloops. John Jacob As
tor came promptly forward two years ago,
after the death of Mr. Goelet, and offfered
cups, thut the race might be perpetuated.
There should certainly be a good race
to-morrow, not only between the two bi^
fliers, b.ut In the schooper class between
the Colonia, Amorlta and Quizetta.
The club has two courses for the race,
depending, at course, on the wind. In
both cases the yachts will start and fin
ish at Krenton's Keef lightship, and each
course is thirty-eight miles long.
The stan will be made at 10:30. the
Bloops betng sent away first, the schooners
following ftve minutes later. The yachts
will sail with time allowance, and there
Is no limit on the race. On Tuesoay the
wind cups will be contested i"'>r. the Co
lumbia and Defender also sailing, and on
Wednesday, after the Owl and Gamecock
colors have been fought f>>r by the crews
of the fleet, the squadron will disband.

most in Their Grasp.
The California Cricket Club added an
other to its list of victories in the matches
for the Brown pennant at Alameda yes
terday. Dickinson won the toss and, be

24 and W. G. Fortmann for 15. The in
