six wickets for 43 runs, and Roman the
remainder for >;.'> runs, bowling with only
one change throughout the innings.

were made by Sloman and Lubbock, the

by Gunn and Schroeder, who carried it
from 78 to 107. Lubbock is an old Etonian

tall, he has a great reach. At the eighth
wicket down thÂ« match was won. and the
ninth man raised the score to 134. Crol!
took three wickets for 33. Peel two for 32

BAKE CLAMS AT TIBURON.
Corinthian Yachtsmen Enjoy a Feast
and a Jolly Aftarnoon.
The Corinthians enjoyed a successful
clambake at Tiburon yesterday. Early in
the morning a great pile of clams, ears of
corn and pieces of chicken had been pre
pared in a hollow of the rocks on the
beach, and at 1 o'clock these were spread
before the hungry yachtsmen. Qeorge
Crowell, who supervised the arrange
ments, was complimented on all sides.
After luncheon a reception committee of
lifty or more, with Prank Thompson as
leader and Douglas ErsKine as standard
bearer, marched down to the depot to
welcome the band of Sequoia Parlor, Na
tive Sons of the Golden West.
Among the old-time yachtsmen and hon
orary members who were present were
I'ncie George Bromley and Charles G.
Yale. The former made one of his char
acteristically happy speeches, which was
enthusiastically received. Maurice Cra
mer presided at the piano, and Frank
Thompson. Cathcart and Ryan sang
"Doc" Emmons recited, and others
'"obliged the company."
The entire fleet of the Corinthians lay at
anchor in the cove, augmented by the
schooners Azalene and Bonita, the yawl
Frolic, the sloop AVill-o'-the-Wisp " and
others. Among the guests were G. T. S
White. J. M. Punnett. ex-Commodores J
\V. Pew. T. F. Tracy and A. J. Young.
The whole affair passed off pleasantly
and. this being the second clambake that
the Corinthians have given at the club
house, it may be considered an- annual
event.
During the afternoon the Breitzmanns'
sloop Cupid left her moorings for a trip
up the river.

NEBRASKANS ARE
FINALLY WORSTED
The baseball game yesterday between
1 teams of the Nebraska volunteers and
the Union Iron Works at the Mechanics'
Institute grounds. Sixteenth and Folsom
streets, was won by the latter by a score
of 20 to 9.
The workers in steel and iron proved too
skillful fur the tall Nebraskans. although
the game put up by the soldiers was a
strong one until the sixth inning. A crowd
over 1000 strong was present and enjoyed a
good afternoon's sport, at the same time
contributing their share to the entertain
ment fund for the California boys. The
receipts could not be accurately ascer
tained yesterday, but will amount to more,
than $100.
Private Grayson. the Nebraska volun
teer who fired the first shot of the war
with the Filipinos, did his best to cheer
his comrades on to victory; but Delmas of
the Union Iron Works team received a
mascot in the second inning that did the
work and hoodooed the Nebraskans. The
mascot in question was a large cabbage,
with Which Uelmas was presented by his
admirers amid the plaudits of the crowd.
The score stood 7 to 7 at the close of
the fifth inning, but in the sixth the local j
team forged rapidly ahead, and when the |
last out was made it stood 20 to 9 in their
favor. The pitching of Shuman of the Ne
braska? and the three-base hits by Bulger
and Crawford of the Union Works were
the noticeable features of the game. The
teams lined up as follows:

Improving a Racetrack.
PLEASANTON; Aug. 13.â The Pleasan
ton racetrack changed hands two weeks
ago, passing into the possession of C B.
Challsworth; an English gentleman.
The new proprietor has just let a con
tract for the building of 250. new stalls
and a fine big clubhouse. The old build
ings now on the property will be torn
down at once to make room for the new
improvemennts.
MANY FEATURES
FOR THE PARADE
The parade rommittce has decided to
make a special feature of a division con
posed ol all honorably discharged soldiers
of the late war. The soldiers will be in
vited to meet at the headquarters tent, in
Union square, at some date to be fixed
by them and select a marshal and two
aids. All who are eligible to participate
in this division are requested to register
at the headquarters so that the commit
tee can reach an early idea of the num
ber that will participate. The soldiers
will he provided with all the necessary
equipments, and those of their number
who are disabled will be furnished with
ciirriaKes.
The Woodmen of the World are very en
thusiastic in the matter or parading, and
will make their first appearance in public
on the day of the reception to the Cali
fornia boys.
Acceptances of the invitation to partici
pate in the big parade are coming in so
rapidly from all quarters that the labor
of arranging the details will require the
closest attention for the next week. Grand
Marshal Pistoles! has issued notice that
all organizations failing to report before

JUMPED IN THE BAY.
John Johnston, a Teamster, At
tempted to Commit Suicide.
John Johnston, a teamster, while suffer
ing from an overindulgence in liquor,
early yesterday morning attempted to
commit suicide by jumping into th>- bay
at the foot of .Mission street He was
i up by a boatman just as !â â 
about to go down for the third time and
Immediately removed to the Receiving
Hospital.
To Dr. McGettigfan Johnston said he hart
been drinking heavily for the past three
weeks, and becoming despondent, prin
cipally due to his failure to gel more
liquor, he determined to end his exist
ence. After having a parting drink with
a stranger whom he met in a saloon,
Johnston walked to the font of Mission
street, and after divesting himself of his
coal threw himself into the bay.
To-day his friends intend to have him
sent to the Home for Inebriates.

KNOCKED DOWN BY A CAR.
Marie Cassina Seriously Injured on
Broadway.
A middle aged woman giving her name
as Marie Cassina was knocked down by
a car at Broadway and Stockton streets
last night anil sustained an ugly scalp
wound and a deep cut over the left eye.
According to the story of the injured
woman she wan crossing Broadway,
intending to visit a friend, when she was
struck by the car and knocked down.
Although bleeding profusely from the
wounds in her head and face, she bravely
walked to her friend's house, to whom
she told the story "f the accident.
A doctor was at once summoned, hut
at his suggestion she was removed to
the Receiving Hospital. Dr. Dray, after
stitching the wounds, had the woman re
tnoved to htr home on Sto.kton street,
between Broadway and Vai

. DOLLAR TAX CRY IS BOGUS
VALUE OF OAKLAND A THING
TO CONJURE WITH.
His Honor's Message on the Tax
Levy Will Probably Recommend
a Medium Between the Dif
ferent Estimates.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
903 Broadway, Aug. 13.
It is time to put on the brakes. The
municipal automobile is going altogether
t<io fast and a wreck appears to be cer
tain. One or two great things can be
accomplished in one fiscal year, but not
a dozen. Oakland cannot stand the high
pressure now being put upon her. New
charters, boulevards, parks, crematories,
water works and a host of other necessi
ties are big projects. They re of more
importance than the rejection of a saloon
license or a conflict between Betty and
the poundman over a cow, or even the
painting of the City Hall steps. They can
only be brought about by the exercise of
tact, money, brains and persistency. There
is a limit to each of these In this city,
that cannot bo exceeded. It now becomes
the duty of the Councilmen to determine
that limit and to be bound by it.
The first consideration, very naturally,
js money, and after that expediency. Mon
day evening the Council will hear what
the Auditor has to say about taxes. Mayor
Snow will also send in an accompanying
message. Mr. Breed's figures have been
printed and circulated. The Mayor's ideas
are yet sealed, but. any one who has fol
lowed his course for the last ten years
need not do any guessing. Mr. Snow's
t message will be as diplomatic as his con
duct' has been Cor the past four months.
He has figured that the total estimates
of the various departments will need a
tax- of 15 cents more than the dollar. The
Auditor has cut everything to get it with
in the dollar. The Mayor will go be
tween the two. ' -It will be found tnat he
has added to th Auditor's ligures some
improvements that are in tne depart
mental estimates.
No one knows better than Mr. Snow the
hollowness of the cry of the dollar tax i
levy. Oakland is now assessed for $6,000,-
OKi less than a few ears ago. On a dollar
tax this decrease means a reduction of
J'JO.iHH). In 1891 the city was assessed for
$1,000,000 more than this year, and no sane
man will believe that Oakland is not
worth $10.<>X>,(XÂ»0 more to-day than in IWI.
At the former date the vast system of
street railroads now in operation was in
its infancy, and many of the roads were
not built. None of the larger business
blocks then existed; Clay street was un
heard of as a commercial thoroughfare
and Washington street was only begin
ning to be a rival to Broadway. Had the
assessment roll kept reasonable pate With
the city's growth, and had now been
alx-ut $lO,WJ,OW, a tax of $1 would be tun
pie for maintaining the city government.
Instead, the city and its needs have
grown and the basis of income has de
creased about 14 per cent. To argue in
favor of a permanent tax levy of $1 under
such circumstances is nothing short of
nonsense. This fact is apparent to both
the Mayor and the Council, and although
there niay be a littif- friction at the open- I
ing of the debate, 1 have little duubt that
t!;.- levy, when finally named, will be ac
ceptable to the taxpayers. I would re
ji.^t what 1 said some weeks ago, that
although at times there might be more
wisdom displayed in the Council cham
ber, there could hardly be more sincerity
and honesty, and consequently the people
believe that they. will receive some benefit
lor every dollar of taxes collected. The
remark last week in a Democratic paper
that â¢"the lower the levy the less the op
portunity for boodling," has no bearing
on the present Council.
There will be one section missing from
the Mayor's message that he was anxious
'.â¢. urgent to the Council. Mr. Snow has
fjr-at faith that President Kipley of the
Santa Fe will promise tv locate a termi
j.ai somewhere â¢within the boundaries of
Oakland. The Mayor has been in touch
with the Santa Fe people for months and
has been waiting for President Kipley,
but as that gentleman has only just ar
rived in the State no promise from him
tan Le included in the. message Monday
nighU
Many of those who should have studied
the city charter as a duty have arrived
at the conclusion that the organic law
of the municipality has a great deal in
it that is good. It could probably be
made a fairly good law if it were amend
ed in about half a dozen places. Some
solution must be found for the intricacies
surrounding the Board of Works. Why
the City Engineer should be one of those
ty control the patronage of the Police,
>ire and Street Departments, and the
City Auditor should have no seat In the
board. Is something that has always re
mained unexplained. This is the hardest
problem that will confront a convention
of freeholders charged with the duty of
charter making or amending. The exact
line separating the jurisdiction of the
board and ill-.- City Council must also be
determined. There are some restrictions
regarding contracts that need to be ad
justed so that the city can take advantage
n f current prices the same as any other
purchaser. Incidentally I would add that
the salary of the Street Superintendent
be increased. There is no reason why this
I'fhcer should be paid less than the Chicf 3
of the Police and Fire Departments. His
iinaiK.-ial responsibilities are greater than
either of these '!.â  .rs and he must be a
man with an engineering education,
whereas the other two offices are fre
quently filled from the political ranks.
The Superintendent of the Street Depart
l ment is underpaid at $1500 per year, while
the Chief of Police receives $2100.
Both the City Attorney and the Mayor
believe that the present charter can be
perfected by 'the necessary amendments,
and as the tax levy estimates make no
appropriation for a charter election this
may be the plan adopted.
"A Chinaman always wants to learn;
he may differ from you, even when he
fully agrees with you for the purpose
of drawing out of you your method of
arriving at your conclusion, which may be
better than his method." This remark
was made Friday night before the Board
nf Trade by non. F. .X. Schoonmaker,
perhaps the most convincing speaker that
ever came from the East to lecture in the
West. His remarks reminded me of the
manner in which Mr. Mott's suggestions
i-gar'ding the City Hall have been re
ceived. Of course they provoked a con
troversy, as does everything in Oakland.
>pinions, columns of them, h-ave appeared
in print, some intelligent, some honest,
but more the stereotyped expression of
the knockers who infest our midst and
who are as suspicious as the Chinese, but
not so sincere. M. J. Keller seems to
have hit the nail on the head by his re
r:iark that the plan of Mr. Mott will ac
complish the long desired opening of
Washington street along the lines of least
resistance. This is all right so far as the
plan is concerned, but by the way the
opposition is showing its teeth Mr. Mott
may have an idea of his own regarding
the Una of least resistance before he gets
Through. Altogether the municipal pro
gramme- insures immunity from ennui till
Christmas, at the earliest.

HEPTUNE was the host of the
Arlons, both of San Francisco
and Gotham, yesterday, and
the hoary old sea king proved
a most amiable host. The
steamer Piedmont left the ferry slip at
9 a. m. with 1200 happy, good-natured
and enthusiastic Germans, including
the visitors from the East, as passen
gers, and two policemen as extra bal
last, bound on an excursion around
the bay. The steamer headed for the
Union Iron Work?, and after circling
around the battleship lowa started for
the briny deep. As the vessel crossed
the line, somewhere in the vicinity of
Angel Island, Neptune, in the person
of E. A. Otto, came aboard, armed
with an enormous trident and a fierce
growth of whiskers and attended by a
body-guard that gave every evidence
of a long residence among barnacles
and seaweed. The crew and passengers
were lined up against the bulwarksâ
and barâ and the ceremony of initia
tion into the mysteries of the deep was
begun. President Richard Weinacht
of the New York Arions was first in
vested with the rank of admiral and
installed as supreme commander of the
ship as a mark of special favor. Presi
dent J. F. Plagemann of the San Fran
cisco Arions was made vice admiral,
and the jeweled collar was placed
about his neck by the graceful maiden,
Delphine, known among her associates
as George Purlenke.
Delphine forgot to shave before
breakfast and was severely rebuked
by his Majesty for appearing in com
pany with a very large mustache dec
orating her upper lip. Fritz Beringer
was made commodore and Dr. Max
Magnus captain of the fleet, and then
the Sea King took his departure, after

of his brother-in-law, M, Gibson, at
Fruit vale last Thursday night. His death
is attributed to stomach troubles. foÂ»
which he sought relief in California's
climate some months ago. He was ag*ed
74 years. The remains will be shipped to
Wisconsin, where the deceased leaves a
widow and several children.
Fred 1-1 all, a son of the deceased, who
is the manager of the Harvey eattng
houses connected with the Santa Fe line,
thought to surprise his father by an un
expected visit. It had been impossible to
reach him by telegraph, and he did not
know of his father's death until apprised
of the fact to-night on his arrival.

NORTON'S DRUNK
SAVES HIM FROM
SUICIDE'S DEATH
Cuts Neck and Wrist
With a Razor.
BLOOD IS CLOGGED BY WHISKY
SLUGGISH CIRCULATION PROVES
A PRICELESS BOON.
Surgeons Sew Up the Gashes and
and the Patient Concludes He
Will Tarry on Earth
Awhile Longer.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, Aug. 13.
Crazed with liquor and courting death
because he Imagined he had outlived nU
usefulness and was bereft of friends, \VU
Ham Norton, aged 60 years, cut his throat
i and right wrist with a razor at Decoto
shortly after midnight last night and laid [
down beside a hayrick to calmly wait lor
the end to pome. ,
The razor iaiied to cut an artery, and
i when he- awakened from a stupor at sun
' rise this mornint Norton found him.selr
wallowing in a pool of blood. He con
cluded he did not want to die, and with
the little strength left him he hastened
to town, where a friend, after binding
his injuries with towels, furnished him
with a ticket to Oakland. .
On his arrival at the Receiving Hospital
Norton made known to Warden Pau. hla
condition and then collapsed. Dr. Strat
ton was promptly summoned and an In
vestigatlon Bhowed that Norton had cut
a gash two inches lung on the right r.ide :
of the neck, barely missing the jugular:
on the left side of th>- neck was another |
I gush about an inch and a half in length |
while the incision on the right wrist- had I
severed the tendon of flexor carpi radians,
bo that it necessitated the extension of
the wound two inches in order that Dr.
Stratton might reach the end with his
small forceps to connect the severed ton
don. . , ,
The gashes on the neck each required
i four or live stitches, a,nd the remarkable
vitality of the would-be suicide will carry
him through.
Norton's miraculous escape from death j
' is t'ne subject of wonderment with the
physielaris, who are inclined to the be
! lief that the only accountable reason for
his ir,; bleeding t" < i â¢ ; 1 1 1 1 is the fact that
liis debauch had so seriously affected the
circulation of his blood that it became too
. 6lUgglÂ£ h.
"Yes. whisky caused me to try to put
an end to my life, and whisky also de
feated my very purpose," said Norton this
evening. ""I had been drinking very much
for the past few days and I was ready !
to die. bÂ«t death didn't seem to be reedy
for me. I have no further jntention of j
attempting to commit suicide."
Norton formerly lived at San Diego, but
of late years he has found steady empioy
ni. in with the Spring Valley Water Com
pany. He is unmarried.
FOUL WORD NEARLY
COST HIM HIS LIFE
OAKLAND, Aug. 13.â The utterance of
a vile epithet by Fetix Jacoby of Piert
mont, addressed to C. H. McKinnon,
nearly cost the former his life this morn
ing. Jacoby is a gardener, aged 40 years,
and McKinnon by occupation Is a tailor.
The two men met in a saloon this morn
ing find afterward quarreled in front of
the <;nlindo Hotel on .Eighth street. One
word l<-d to another, and finally Jacoby
called McKinnon a vile name. -vlcKinnon
eel :; by striking Â£Ke gardener a
blow between the eyes with his list,
which knocked him down. As he fell his
head struck, upon the concrete curb. ren~
(!< ring him unconscious. McKinnon was
arrested and charged with battery.
Jacoby was treated a 1 the Receiving
Hospital fÂ»r a badly lacerated scalp. H.Â«
was unconscious for several hours, and
though his injury will not prove fatal
from present indications, it is feared that
his I. rain lias been affected. He imagines
that an Kighth-street car struck him,
and in his delirium he declares he will
sue the Oakland Transit Company for
damages.

OVER WITH BURNS

Go Unpledged to the Demo
cratic Convention.
_ .
Assemblyman Henry C. Dibble called
upon Colonel Burns at the Palace last
night, and some further discussion con- j
cerning an extra session of the Legislature j
ensued between them. Phil Crimmins â¢
made the third party to the powwow,
but for all that nothing more than was
published yesterday was accomplished.
Although Crimmins and Kelly showed
up poorly tn the late primary elect ions
they still control the Senatorial votes of
Assemblyman â¢ - Mike" Barry and Fred
I^undqiiist. and, it is said, can deliver the !
ballots of Assemblyman Crowley an.l Sen
ator Feeney, Democrats, if it cornea to a I
Bhow-down. For this and fur other rea
sons Mr. Crimmins has occupied a prom- '
Inent part in the conferences of the past
few days.
Rather a peculiar aspect of affairs has!
developed among the delegates elected by
the Committee of One Hundred at the pri
maries. Although it has been known that
they were elected by the Phelan push for
Phelan purposes, they have held caucuses
in every district during the past few days
and have decided to go into the conven
tlon pledged to no candidate. In the meet- j
ings that have occurred there have been :
several anti-Phelan harangues of much
force, and the spirit of discord has devel
oped to such an extent that no candidate I
is to be named until thts eve of the con- j
ventlon.
The selection of a county committee at |
tho coming Democratic convention prom- ;
Ises to be a lively affair. In every district
where the Committee of One Hundred is
in power there are candidates paint-" fur
the places, and it will be no easy task to
settle the rivalry in some districts.
Ralney will probably win some in the
Thirty-eighth District, and George Max
well is willing to do the right itiing for â 
the Bush street boss in the Forty-fourth i
District. Outside of these districts Rain, y'â 
at present he is not so strong, but he is
working hard to win a few more on the '
list.
Delegates elected to the various conven
tions can receive their certificates of eloc
tion by calling at the registration office I
on Monday after 11 o'clock a. m.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL.
FORECAST.
In the upper portion of the Sacramento Val
i,. Oregon. Washington and Idaho the weather
i is generally cloudy and threatening. Light rain
has fallen from Cape Mendocino northward
along the coast and over Washington and Ore
" An area of low pressure is central north of
Montana and a trough-like depression extends
southward over the plateau region to Mexico.
The pressure has risen slightly along the coast
and fallen over the interior.
The temperature has fallen decidedly over
Washington. Oregon and Idaho, elsewhere it
has remained nearly stationary.
Conditions are favorable for showers in the
extreme northern portion of California to-night
and In Nevada and Utah Monday.
High win. are reported from Northern Ne
vada, and a thunder storm from Kalis;.--!.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours, ending midnight August 14:
Northern Californiaâ Showers in the extreme
northern portion -night; partly cloudy in
north portion; fair in south portion Monday;
fresh northwest wind.
Southern Californiaâ Fair Monday; fresh west
wind. 'â¢". '
Nevada- Cloudy, with showers Monday; cool
er in northeast portion.
Ttuhâ Cloudy, with showers Monday; cooler.
Arizonaâ Partly cloudy Monday.
San Francisco and vicinityâ Partly cloudy
Monday: fresh to brisk west wind.

NOTE â In the above exposition of the tides
I the early mornlnjj tides are given in the ieft
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column Elves the second tide of
I the day, the third time column the third tide
I and the last or right hand column gives the
I lust tide of the day, except when there are but
i three tide?, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â 1 precedes the height,
and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the lower low waters.

CITY WILL BE IN
A BLAZE OF GLORY
Electric Display Planned
on a Most Gigantic
Scale.
SAN FRANCISCO will literally hÂ» in a
Maze of glory when the current is
sent through the 10,000 Incandescent
electric lights that jro to make up the
great electrical illumination which will be
â¢ Lture of the celebration In honor of
the return of the California volunteers.
Nothing like it has ever been seen in
this city. The citizens who have wa
with interest t>s - â  stringing of wires
across Market street can form but
a feeble conception of the magnitude of
the vi ale of grandeur
h the display will be made.
Scarcely â¢ I the wires for the
street lights have been put in place.
There ha.s been a vague idea that Market
â  was to be illuminated overhead, but
the plans of the decoration committee are
much more pretentious. There will be a
continuous stream of electric lights from!
the time ball en the tower of the ferry |
depot up Market street to the dome, of the
city Hall.
The committee, consisting of W. J. Mar
tin (chairman). William H. Mills, I. \V.
Hellman Jr., A. Schilling, Charles Hirscta
and H. M. Holbrook, had many proposi
tions under consideration and spent many |
days discussing them before they finally :
decid 'I upon the electrical display mid
awarded the contract to the Pacific [ncan
desi 'I.; Lamp < Jompany.
.The plans call for an Illuminatidn the
equal of which ha never been seen on
the Pacific Coast. It will be on a
nf grandeur never before undertaken in .
this oiiy and of a magnitud â¢â â¢> can
appr< ciate from a casual Inspection of the
wÂ«.rk thus far prosecuted.
T.-n thousand in ni lights will be
The stringers which will form, the
canopy of light over Market street will ;
consume In the neighborhood of 216,000 feet <
hi copper wire. En the decorations at the!
ferry depol and the City Hall 26,000 feet
of Insulated wire will be necessary, and
every fool of this hitter will have to be j
handled separately by the workmen in the 1
employ of the contractors. So great has
been the demand for electric material to
complete the display that the local mark!
has been drained. Los Angeles and Sao
ramento have been (ailed upon for ex
tras, and all the available stock in the
two cities has been spoken for. Even
with the supplies from the outside cities
the Pacific Company found itself short
nearly 5000 porcelain sockets for the In
candescent lamps. Tn order that there
might be no delay In completing the work
the company improvised :i Bocket out of
copper wire and yesterday afternoon set a
large force of men at work turning them
out. Eighty men are now employed bj
the company exclusively on the work in
connection with the illumination, and as
soon as they begin to string the wires
on ihr ferry* depot and the City Hall an
additional crew of twenty-five men will
be pressed into service. Their storeroom
on Anna lane Is a scene of unusual activ
ity. Shifts are at work day and night
preparing the material for the men on the !
streets. The work is being prosecuted :
with vigor, and despite the short time j
remaining for its? completion everything
will be ready when the California boys ar
rive.
Beginning at the ferry, the tower of the
building will be outlined by strings of
light extending from the time ball down
to :i ir> big clock. Pour huge electric stars i
about ten feet high will shine from the
four sides of the tower midway between
the pinnacle and the clock. Just above
the hip timepti cc, on the Market-street
side, there will be a set figure about 15x25
feet with the words "All Welcome Home."
T'p Market street to the City Hall lights
will be strung across the street at inter
vals of fifty feet. Each of these strings
will carry twenty-four lights, and every
fifth cluster will be arched with thirty
four lights.
The decorations at the City Hall will be
r.nf of the features of the ilumination.
The building from the base of the globe
on the dome to the foundation will be
brought out in profile. Every outline will
be marked by electric lights. From the
main columns strings of lights will ex
tend to the dome. A huge canopy of
lights with the Lick monument as the
center will be constructed over City Hall
Park,

PROGRAMME WILL
BE READY TO-DAY
A Few More Seats May Be
Had lor Thursday's
Benefit.
The theatrical managers have ad
dressed a complimentary letter to Mayor
Phelan stating that a vote of thanks has
been tendered him for his, ardent efforts
in furthering the success of the Cali
fornia Volunteers' theatrical benefit fund.
Th< Mayor has acknowledged th< receipt
Of the letter with thanks.
The theatrical managers will meet in
the Office Of the Columbia Theater this
morning at ll o'clock and arrange furthei
details for the benefit at the Orpheum
next Thursday. The programme will be
F^^^fniÃifsajsafÃiPaPaisaPsPSi
P, . . *-, Â» âº ... -, * - - - *
completed and given to the public to
morrow. A number of surprises have
been prepared, and the aggregation of
talent will be the strongest that has ever
appeared on any stage at one time in this
city.
The management desires it to be known
that all unsold tickets will be placed on
sale this morning at the Orpheum box
office at 9 o'clock. Notwithstanding the
enormous sale there are a few seats left.
Bids will also be received by L. A.
Morgenstern for two unsold boxes, one
of which was donated by Edward Mar
shuts, who paid his â¢ premium of $100.
Wendell Easton has consented to sell thÂ«
box at the Produce Exchange or the
Chamber of Commerce, and if he can rind
the time he will do so. However, bids
may be left- at the Orpheum office.
The management also requests all those
having seats at the benefit who may wish
to leave before the end of the extremely
long performance, to deposit their scat
coupons in the box-office and they will he
resold for the benefit of tlie fund. The
performance will last six hours in all,
commencing at 12:30 p. m. sharp. .
The managers will themselves consti
tute the corps of ushers, and will have
twenty assistants from the various
theaters of the city.

MEMORIAL SERVICES.
The Red Cross Society is arranging
services in memory of the dead of the
California regiment. Ellinghouse & Mott
have donated the use of the, Alhambra
Theater for tlie purpose, and the date has
been sel for Sunday, September 3. Among
th. speakers will he Dr. Jordan of Stan
ford. President Benjamin Ide Wheeler of
Berkeley, Rev. Robert Mackenzie and Rev.
.1. Cherlngton. The musical features will
bo of an exceptional nature. The Loring
Club has volunteered to fill out several of
the numbers, and it is stated that this
will !Â»<â¢ the first time that the club has
ever suns ;it any public affair. The ar
rangements are In charge of Mrs. Moore
of the Red Cross Society.
